Minister Conger's statement that it would be certain death
for the Ministers to leave Peking confirms the belief of mem
bers of the Cabinet.
The dispatch no longer leaves any question as to the atti-.
tude of the Chinese Government. Imperial trops are openly
firing upon the legations. There is evidently no attempt on the
part of the Chinese authorities- to keep up the fiction that the
Ministers are being attacked by the insurgents, Further; than
this, the Minister is evidently firmly convinced that the; imperial
Government, in wishing to have the inmates of the legation start
from Peking, is plotting to have them murdered on the way to
Tientsin.
1A/ILL SPUR ON POWERS.
This dispatch will serve to spur the powers onwafd'to
more determined efforts to reach Peking .without The
message just as it was received from Minister Conger will be
communicated to all. the powers; to-morrow and copies will be
cabled to Rear Admiral Remey and Major General Gha'ffe*
While some members of the Cabinet desire a declaration of
war, at least one of the parties to the conference to-night frowns
upon the suggestion. He said that he did not see how the
Conger message changed the situation in the slightest degree.
Of course proof of the duplicity of the Chinese Government is
furnished, but because the Chinese are making war upon the
The President will sleep on the Conger dispatch to-night,
and to-morrow he is expected to communicate his views to the
State Department, when any changes in the policy of the ad
ministration will be put into effect.
DUPLICITY IS PROVED.
It has been stated time and again by high officials of the
administration that this Government will be the last to declare
war on China, and. notwithstanding the indubitable evidence
now to hand of the trickery which the Chinese Government con
templated in order to lure foreign representatives to their death
no action has been taken looking to such a declaration.
At the same time the question of policy necessarily must be
considered, and as there is nothing to be gained by a formal
declaration, but instead the precipitation of additional compli
cations, it is unlikely that the President will adopt such a course.
For more than two hours these officials discussed the sit
uation with the President, and just before Secretary Foot
ended the interview Adjutant General Corbin, who had been
summoned, arrived to discuss the military aspect of the situa
tion with the Secretary of War and through him with the Presi
dent. Some of the President's counselors are strongly in favor
of calling Congress together for the purpose of making a for
mal declaration of- war. Those officials have come to the con
clusion that they have ample ground for such action in case it is
deemed advisable.
The dispatch from Minister Conger was immediately tele
phoned to the President. There was some talk between the
President and Colonel Montgomery, who telephoned for Mr.
Adee regarding the international phase of the Conger dis
patch, and when Secretary Root arrived the President talked
with him as to the effect of the dispatch both upon this country
and upon other powers.
WOULD DECLARE WAR.
Although the authorities have had ever}' reason to believe
the Chinese Government was responsible for the condition of af
fairs in Peking, it has tacitly accepted the polite fiction that a
mob was in control and the imperial authorities were unable to
regain control. Minister Conger's latest dispatch dissipates
any such belief. He speaks of "the Chinese. Government in
sisting upon our leaving Peking, which would be certain
death," and then of "rifle firing upon us daily by imperial
troops." Taken with the previous information received by the
authorities, there can no longer be any doubt that the Chinese
Government is responsible for the existing disorders. It is
stated with emphasis to-night that the President purposes to
hold that Government to strict accountability for events in Pe
king.

l j INGTON, Aug. 7. â Not only are the imperial troops
again attacking the foreign Ministers in Peking, but the
Chinese Government seems to be actually conniving to bring
about their assassination. These facts were presented in the
cipher dispatch received by the State Department this after
noon from Minister Conger and made public late to-night. The
message was promptly telephoned to the President. While no
action has been taken, it is stated on good authority that the
United States is nearer declaring war on China at this moment
than it has been since the Ministers were cut off from commu
nication.

CHEFTT. Aug. 6.â The allies attacked
the Peitsang lntrenchments on both sides
of the Pelho River yesterday morning and
drove out the Chinese. When forced from
their outer works* the Chinese blew up the
bridge. : The Japanese crossed the river,
exposed to a murderous cross fire, losing
heavily.
The fighting lasted from 3 o'clock until
11. with heavy losses on both sides. The
Chinese retreated up the river, pursued
by the allies, but the pursuit was diffi
cult, as the river banks had been cut and
the ground was flooded.
There is a force of about 15,000 Chinese
about two days' march to the south and
east of Tientsin, which la guarded by 6000
men of the allied forces with fourteen
guns.
An Imperial edict has been Issued at
Peking that the Tientsin and Taku forts
must be retaken at any cost. ,-- : =r

THE RAILROAD BRIDGE AT TANGTSUN DESTROYED BY THE CHI
NESE IN JUNE.
Tangtsun is a considerable town lying on the east side of the Peiho. twelve
miles beyond Peitsang and seventeen miles from Tientsin. There the Impe
rial Railroad crosses the Peiho and strikes off more to the west toward Pe
kLng. The important steel bridge over which it runs was destroyed by the
Chinese during Admiral Seymour's advance to the relief of Peking in June
As the railroad can Boon be repaired that far, it will be used as an ad
vanced base of supplies for the allied forces, A large force of Chinese are
said to be strongly fortified there, and a heavy battle may be expected before
the allies gain possession.

With Reinforcements Now on the

Special Dispatch to The Call.
ALL BUREAU, WELLINGTON HOTEL V WASHINGTON, Aug. 7.â Word has been received that Japan
reinforced her troops in China by 3OOO infantry and one battery of siege artillery. These
will bring her army up to a total of more than 25,000 men and make it the strongest of any power in
China. The allied forces on Chinese soil, when the additional Japanese troops and the troops of other powers
arrive, will number 100,000 men.
So far from any objection being made by any government to Japan's increase of her military strength.
in China, the powers rather welcome it. The stout resistance the Chinese are making furnishes good grounds
for sending such a force to China that the Celestial troops will be unable to stop their advance. European
nations agree with the "United States that the defeat of the allies by the Chinese would do incalculable harm
and are as anxious that the troops shall capture Peking without reverse as this Government is.
Major Byron, chief quartermaster on General Chaffee's staff, has wired to Colonel Miller, chief quar
termaster at Manila, that he has plenty of lighters and is getting supplies ashore at Taku, but he needs mors
tugs to move the lighters about the harbor. Colonel Miller has sent several tugs to Taku and others will
be hastened there. !
The Sedgwick, bringing home a battalion of the Eleventh Infantry and a battalion of the Fifth Cav
alry, which will be sent to China, left San Juan to-day for New York.
HONGKONG, Aug. 7.â Extensive arrangements are being made for accommodating more military In
Hongkong. The country adjacent is perfectly quiet, although it is deemed prudent to withdraw the mis
sionaries.

THE STORY OF

How He Came to Give Notes
for Large Sums to the
Slovinskis.
Sketches in l>etail the Sensational
Scene in the Parlor of
His Home at Los
Angeles.
Special Dispatch to The CalL
LOS ANGELES, Aug. 7.â The prelimi
nary examination of John Slovinski and
wife on a charge of attempting to levy
$15,000 blackmail on Capitalist A. R.
Maines of this city was begun before Jus
tice Austin this morning. Maines, who
was the principal witness, was called and
told of his experience with the Jealous
husband on July 20 last.-
Maines said that he first met Mrs. Slo
vlr.skl while she was stopping at the Gar
vanza Hotel about the middle of last
February, this year. I He saw her but a
few times, as the hotel closed soon after
this and all the guests left. He next saw
her and met her husband for the first
time at the Sea Beach Hotel at Catalina
I July 8. He was at the island on a busi-
I ness trip and saw Mrs. Slovinski on the
plazzl. He had afterward gone into the
hotel and there Mrs. Slovinski had In
i troduced him to her husband. There was
some general conversation for five or ten
minutes and he had left. Later Mrs. Slo
vinski had met him near the bathhouses
and warned him csainst her husband,
telling him not to see her again or speak
I to her, 39 her husband was jealous and
I had made threats to kill him.
The next time he saw the couple was
July 13. when they called at his home on
Broadway. Here Slovinski said to Maines:
"You have rulr.rd my life and disgraced
my child. I've left my wife penniless on
the island and will never go back to her
or see her again."
Maines said that he denied the charge,
when Slovinski' produced a letter and
asked him why. if he had not been frlend
lv with his wife, he had written It.
Maines said that he did not take the let
ter nor pay any attention to It. for the
reason that Slovinski had told him that
It was a copy of the original, which he
intended to have lithographed and sent
broadcast over the country, to his wife's
rel3tl% - es and those of Maines. \
The next time he paw the Slovinskls,
Maines said, was on the morning of July
20, between S and 9 o'clock, when the
couple walked into his store on Spring
street together and Slovinski said that he
had come to have that letter explained.
Maines told the two that his wife was
away from home, so they adjourned to
his home on South Broadway. On en
tering the house Maines went to the win
dow to raise a curtain, leaving Slovinski
and his wife sitting in chairs near the
folding doors. As he turned toward the
two Slovinski was pointing a revolver at
him. The husband said: "My wife has
confessed everything and I have deter
mined to kill you."
"His hand was shaking so, I was afraid
that the thing might go off unintention
ally," said Maines. "but I told him that
if he -was determined to kill mo, and I
knew he could, there was only one request
that I had to make, and that was for him
to go to the telephone and call up my
wife, that I may say goodby."
" TCo. you don't catch me that way,'
roared Slovinski," continued Maines,
"and then he bogan stamping on the floor,
calling out, 'Confess it, confess it. My
wife has confessed everything. Sit down
or I will kill you -where you stand.' "
Maines said that he thought that It was
time to sit down and did so, and 'then Mrs.
Slovinski fell upon her knees, betting
him to confess, paying that her husband
would kill him if .he did not. She said
that he must not think that she had any
thing to do with this, as her husband had
made her do it. Slovinski, Maines said,
kept on accusing him and he had finally
replied: " 'Well, I suppose your wife has
told the truth." and that is all the confes
sion I made. Slovinski quieted down after
this somewhat, remarking: *Now w there is
Just thla much about it. You must give
me a deed to the Garvanza Hotel and
JjiiOO in cash or your life.' Thinking to
gain time I replied,, 'Isn't this a little ex
travagant? Won't the deed to the hotel
be enough?' But he again became vio
lent and the talk continued."
Malnes continued his testimony up to
the point where he gave the two notes for
SlO.OtX) and 55000 respectively, and then he
was withdrawn to erlve Professor Green
of Colton and Mrs. Thompson of tlie same
place a chance to tell about a quarrel be
tween the Slovlnskis at Catalina on the
night of July 11. when Slovinski beat his
wife to make her confess to relations with
Maines.
The case was then continued until to
morrow morning, when the defense say
they will bring out some startling develop
ments en cross-examination.

REDWOOD CITY, Aug. 7.â The Board
of Trustees of this place are planning ex
tensive improvements between now and
the rainy season. About J?OÂ» will be

ter mains and many of the streets will be

to be made were discussed. In the mac
adamizing of the streets It Is the de
sire of the board to have the propÂ«"Â«ty

the necessity of resorting to the Vrooman
act. Already a large proportion of the
owners of property on a number of the
streets have signified a willingness to do
the work.

WOMAN'S BODY
ADRIFT NEAR
WALDO POINT
SANTA CRUZ. Aug. 7.â Arnos K.
Ziegler, residing at Doyle Gulch,
seven miles from this city, last
night murdered his wife by cut
ting her throat ar.d then at
tempted suicide by slashing at his own
throat, and though he made several ugly
gashes he failed in his intentions. He is
now In the County Jail.
The terrible tragedy occurred at mid
night, and was tho outcome of an un
happy marriage and domestic Infelicity.
There were no witnesses to the deed; no
one save Ziegler can tell of the woman s
frantic struggles in the road in front of
her home, when her blood-lusting husband
overtook the fleeing woman and did his
murderous work; and when her brother
in-law, who resided in the same house,
rushed out In response to her cries, he
met the murderer with his hands red with |
his victim's blood. Asking Ziegler where
his sister-in-law was. he received tho re
ply:
"She will never come back. You need
not call the officers, as I am going to cut
my throat."
First News of the Crime,
It was 12:30 this morning that Sheriff
Besse was aroused at his home in this
city by Attorney \V. B. Sharp of San
Francisco, a brother of Mrs. Ziegler. ar.d
informed of the tragedy in Doyle Gulch.
With Deputy Sherifta Mullen and Alzina
and Coroner Morgan the Sheriff drove out
to Doyle Gulch. As they neared the Zieg
ler home one of the horses, snorting with
fear, sharply swerved to one side. One
of the Sheriffs men got out of the con
veyance, and lighting a match beheld the
bodv of the woman, face downward, the
head almost severed from the body. In the
middle of the road. Her clothing was
drenched with blood and the dust in th<?
road was red. There Â¦were no signs of a
strupglr* there, and the woman had evi
dently never moved after falling.
The Sheriff's men covered the body with
a cloth and went to the farmhouse. On
the kitchen porch they saw a recumbent
form. It was Ziegler, with a slash across
his throat. He was breathing hard and
was conscious, but would not reply to tho
questions put to him. Coroner Morgan,
on examining the wound, found it extend
ed from ear to ear. but was not deop
enough to be dangerous. The doctor
dressed the wound and Ziegler was
covered with blankets. His hands were
still red. ar.d under his body the officers
found the butcher-knife with which he

Amos K. Ziegler, a rancher, living in Doyles Gulch, seven miles
from Santa Cruz, killed his -wife Monday midnight in the road in front
of his home, when she attempted to leave the house because of a quar
rel. He stabbed her with, a butcher knife, and when she fell ha cut her
throat, to "put her out of her misery," as he confessed yesterday. He
coolly admitted that he had made preparations to kill her because she
did not love him and they were always quarreling. After killing her
he attempted to commit suicide by cutting his throat, but" his nerve
failed him.

Both the boys were fully cognizant of
the plot for several days preceding Its
execution, and their stories of the awful
crime show that Fred Himes, who was
supposed to have made a full confession,
told the officers cunning lies in order to
shield himself.
Tho statements of the boys were ob

Church to-day after Deputy Sheriff John
White had arrested Elmer Martin for
complicity in the plot. Frankie Himes
had been in Jail for two or three days,
but the officers wanted them both before
they took a statement from either.
Frankie was the flrst examined. He
said he knew of the plot for about four
days before the fatal attempt was made.
His brother first tried to kill tho senior
Himes by giving him a potion made by
boiling weeds which he had heard were
poisonous.

the sick man ar.d added a teaspoonful of
the ratsbane In the presence of his
brother.
"If the other dose' did not fix him,"
said he, as he emptied the powder
into the pot, "by , this will."
Frankie denied that his father had been
cruel to Fred. He said his brother never
complained of Ill-treatment until after he
had administered the poison. He wanted

DEVASTATION BY FIRE
IN YELLOWSTONE PARK
Worst Conflagration Ever Known in
That Begion Is Sweeping the
Timber Belt.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BUTTE, Mont.. Aug. 7.â Deputy United
States Marshal Sam Jackson. Just In from
Mammoth Hot Springs, reports a destruc
tive forest nre that is sweeping the tim
bered area between the Upper Geyser Ba
sin and the lake in the Yellowstone Na
tional Park. The flro started Friday and
was soon beyond the control of the sol
diers and roail crews, all of whom were
hurried to the scene. The buildings at
the Upper Geyser Basin are in danger.
The line of tiro is ten miles long ana is
spreading rapidly. It is not known how
the fire started, but it is presumed to have
been the work of some careless camper.
Jackson .said to-day:
It would take all the men in Montana and
Wyoming to put out the nre. When I left it
was said that the fire line extended for ten
miles and was undoubtedly spreading. The
timber in that region Is dense and the trees
are of pretty pood size. It is unusually dry
and the wind fairly sweeps thÂ« (lames through
the tops of the big pines. The timber on th<j
continental divide Is heavier than in most sec
tions of the park, and the fear is expressed that
the fire will sweep through a gTeat portion of
it. The buildings at the Upper Geyser Basin
were in danger from. the fire. If the fire is not
checked before it reaches the timber surround
ing the b3sln the buildings will surely be
burned. The big hotel, near Old Faithful Gey
ser, was burned several years ago, but there
are a /lumber of bulldlntcs there still. DensÂ»
clouds of smoke hang over that part of the
park. Everything Is being done that can be
done but men are powerless before such a fire
as this The deer and elk were running out of
the threatened districts. No one knows the ex
tent of the fire, but it is the worst one ever
known in this reclon.
Mr Jackson said that Banker Piper of
St. Marys, Ohio, who strayed from the
Fountain Hotel one week from to-day,
has not been found. A number of sol
diers had been searching for him, but
when the fire broke out between the lake
and the Upper Geyser Basin all of the
soldiers were called In and sent to that
I place.

When Fred was first arrested and made
hi3 confession a good many pitied him.
supposing that his stories of Inhuman
treatment at the hands of his father were
true and that he administered the poison
in a tit of desperation. The two confes
sions received to-day throw a different
light on the crime.
Himes will probably die before morning.
He sank rapidly to-day and is breathing
heavily. Dr. Davidson says his death Is
only a Question of hours.
Martin says he saw the first and second
doses of poison administered. He then
got scarce!, and after advlslns Fred not
to give his father any more of the stuff,
he ran away. The boy corroborated
Frankie regarding Illraes treatment of
his eldest son, which he said was not
cruel.
Martin was to pay Fred $73 for a half
interest on the outfit and had $15 ready to
turn over when Himes died. Fred was to
live with Martin and his parents, and as
compensation for his board was to bring
with him the household furniture. They
were to do teaming and Job work gener
ally, and share the profits equally.
Elmer Martin declares that he
knew of the plot from the first. He
and Fred Himes wanted to get the
latter's father out of the way so that
they could go into partnership with,
the wagon and horses as capital.
to desperation by the parent's ill-treat
ment of him.
The shocking story of moral depravity revealed by the confession
of the Fresno boy, Frederich Himes, who poisoned his father because,
as he alleged, the latter mistreat3d him, is augmented by the confessions
of the boy's accomplices â his younger brother Frankie and his 10-year
old cousin, Elmer Martin. They yesterday told a Deputy Sheriff how the
plot had been laid to poison the senior Himes, not because he ill-treated
Fred, but because they wanted to secure his wagon and horses and go
into the teaming business. They told also of the heartless manner in
which they planned the father's removal. Ths story is almost without
parallel in criminal history.

Monterey Bay's Unknown Dead.
SALINAS, Aug. 7.â Monterey Bay ha3
again given up the body of an unknown
man and there Is much conjecture here
to-night as to whether the man was
murdered, committed suicide or was ac
cidentally drowned.
The body, badly decomposed, was
washed upon the beach near Moss
ing, a few miles from here, to-day. It
was found by lishermen. The head was
badly mutilated, while both hands and
forearms were gone. The man must have
b?en about five feet ten Inches tall and
wquld weigh between 160 and ISO pounds.
The remains were clad In a blue flannel
shirt, brogans and home-knit gray socks.
An Inquest was held and the verdict of
the Coroner's jury was to the effect that
"a person unknown came to his death
from unknown causes."

Leading Sports of the Day
FLYCASTERS BID
ADIEU TO THEIR
TWO CHAMPIONS
Wish W. D. Mansfield and
A. E. Lovett God- "
speed.
Are to Compete in the World's
Tournament at Chicago as
Representatives of the
Local Club.
?
The members of San Francisco Flycast
ing Club met last night at the banquet
board to wish godspeed to Walter D.
Mansfield and A. E. Lovett, who will rep
resent them in the open to the world tour
nament at Chicago. This will bo held on
the 17th and 18th lnst., and will attract
the most expert rod wielders throughout
America.
Three years ago Mr. Mansfield attended
a similar tournament and swept every
thing before him, proving the most expert
of all those in attendance and winning the
titlf of world's champion. Since then the
Eastern men have practiced the art of
competitive flycasting assiduously, re
ceiving most of their education from the
enthusiasts on this coast. As a sequence
they are equaling in practice the beat per
formances of the local men, and the out
come of the tournament is problematic at
the present time. There is no method of
comparison which can be applied, hence
only the test of competition can determine
whether the repressntatlves of this coast
can hold their own with the men who will
come out of the East.
The trophy offered by the San Francisco
Flycasting Club to be competed for at
the Chicago tournament was exhibited at
the dinner. Tt Is a beautiful gold match
box studded with diamonds and artistic
ally enameled with favorite files of the
anglers. It is offered for the best general
average In accuracy at fly and bait cast-
Ing. It was especially offered for competi
tion among members of the Chicago Club,
but they insisted upon its bein,; open to all
competitors. Its inscription reads:
CHICAGO FLYCASTING CLUa.
WORLD'S TOURNAMENTS
1900.
SAN FRANCISCO FLYCASTING CLUB TRO

WELCH TO REFEREE THE
MURPHY-TREMBLE FIGHT
The O'Kane challenge cup, which is now
held by Dr. George \V. Kleiser, will in all
probability be put up as an additional
trophy by the Golden Gate Park Driving
Association for one of the three races to
be run at Tanforan Park on its opening
day, September 24. Dr. Kleiser captured
the beautiful cup at the last meeting of
the club with his fast trotter Alto Vela.
It is usual for the winner of the cup to
hold it one year, but the members of the
Driving Association are in favor of hav
ing it contested for next month.
The matter was informally discussed
after the meeting of the organization last
night, but it will be brought before the
members at the next meeting, which will
be held on Tuesday evening next.
The offer of the Tanforan Park Racing
Association to run three races for the
members of the driving club was accepted
at last night's meeting. Steps were im
mediately taken tx> secure entries and pre
liminaries were arranged. As an induce
ment to the members who have fast trot
ters, the club voted to add $50 additional
to the $.'J00 offered by (he racetrack man
agers. The races will be for the free for
all. 2:20 and 2:30 classes. They will be
run In heats, and winners must land three
out of five to take the money. The purses
will be divided at 50, 30 and 20 per cent.
Entries for tho races will close next Tues
day night at 10 o'clock. The classification
committee will then take action on those
submitted.
The officers of the Golden Gate Park
Driving Association are: President, J. C.
Klrkpatrick; first vice presidium, E. Aigel
tlnger: second vice president. J. C. Oh
landt; treasurer, E. Stewart; secretary,
II. F. Patrick. * i

ARNOLD MUST
FACE CREDITORS
IN OLD LONDON
"We do not take that exact view of the
case at this time, but we havo some ideas.
The most emphatic is that if we should
undertake to make the radical change-s
called for by the I.os Angeles business
men it would be but a matter of a short
time when we would be called down by the
Jobbers of this city and asked that there
should be another adjustment. On that
basis It would be a see-saw and there
would be no satisfaction in the situation
for anybody. The gentlemen from Los
Angeles most certainly have observed the
fact that it is all up and down hill work
from their city and that from San Fran
cisco to Bakersfield the roadbed is prac
tically level. The difference in the cost
of transportation can be readily recog
nized. Topographical conditions regulate
these matters generally, for we cannot
make level plains out of mountains. This
subject has been discussed before, but
this Is the first time the Angelenos have
come to this city to present their claims.
We can see no reason why there should
be any radical change from the existing
schedule, and I do not think there will
be."
"1 was present at the conference of the
Los Angeies business men In which they
usked lor a readjustment of the freight
rates between San Francisco and l^os
Angeles," said Assistant Traffic Manager
Bissell of the Santa fe Company yester
day. "The problem is dittlcuit, and as a
matter of fact we have not considered it
seriously up to this date. It will be given
consideration, however, and I think the
reply will be forthcoming in a few days.
At the present time 1 cannot state what
the nature of the answer will be, but 1
can see no probability of a change from
existing conditions."
"We are placed between two antagonis
tic positions," said Freight Tratnc Man
ager Sproule. "The existing tariff is
based on the lines of business not only of
the shipper but of the carrier. On the
face of the proposition it would appear
that the San Francisco jobbers were fa
vored as against those of Los Angeles,
but when the shlppeqf of the southern
city come to us and asK for a mileage pro
rata we cannot see our way clear to grant
the request. Bakersfield is the main mar
ket they havo In sight, and their plea Is
that as Los Angeles is so near to the
southern end of the San Joaquin Valley
freight rates should be so adjusted as to
give the preference to the metropolis of
the south.
The Angelenos have their eyes on the
region mentioned, and knowing that there
is a large and growing trallic In that sec
tion they are out for it. As a consequence
they have made matters very disagree
able for the men who boss the freight
trains on the two lints Â¦mentioned. The
trouble arises out of the fact that the de
mands of the business men of the south
appear to be reasonable. Tney want a pro
raia mileage arrangement, but under ex
isting conditions matters cannot be i ar
ranged to the satisfaction of the parties
The freight traffic managers of the
Southern Pacific and Santa Fe companies
are not in a happy frame of mind as a re
sult of the visit of the committee of Los
Angeles business men who are here to
fight for the general business^ of the
Southern San Joaquin Valley. /,

VI3ALIA. Aug. 7.â The Visalia and Tu

has been a losing proposition.

Sheriff Taylor's Posse Is Said to Be
on the TTail of the Manslayer
and His Guide.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
COVELO, Aug. 7.â Sheriff Taylor's posse
took up the trail of murderer Kins at
daylight this morning. This information
reached here this evening by one of the
posse, who withdrew owing to a slight in
jury he received by being pulled from his
horse In a thicket. The returning deputy
was unable to outline the route of the
proposed trail to-day, but it is understood
that the posses had divided. One takes
the direct trail in the wake of the fu;ji
tive, while the other posse covers the
background to intercept any doubling by
the murderer. It is feared that this has
been the oversight of the pursuing party
and that King has doubled, thus mislead
ing his pursuers. The inability to get
bloodhounds In this section has retarded
the success of the expedition.
Quite a commontloa was created late
this afternoon by a report that the posse
was now returning with the murderer and
a large number of people gathered on
the streets and awaited the arrival of a
procession of horsemen, but the caval
cade of horsemen proved to be some Gov
ernment agents with a band of horses.
Two trappers coming in from the Mad
River country to-day reported that a
white man and a half-breed Indian called
at their cabin Saturday for food. .They
were afoot and appeared greatly fatigued
and claimed to be prospectors who , had
lost their bearings. When shown a pho
togfaph of King they readily recognized
a close resemblance to the white man
they had seen on Saturday. Everything
points to the assurance that King la
reallv the man being pursued. Â¦-.âÂ¦:

SACRAMENTO, Aug. 7.â About two
weeks ago typical anthrax, or splenic
fever, broke out in the dairy herd on the
Tlttemore ranch, about seven miles up the
river from this city on the Yolo side, and
many of the cattle and several horses
have died. Dr. Megowan, Milk, Food and
Market Inspector of this city, recently
visited the ranch, and after a microscopic
examination of the blood, pronounced the
disease to be anthrax beyond a doubt.
The herd has been vaccinated and quar
antined and it is hoped there will be no
spread of the disease.

C < Sold by all drngsiots. ?Uncle Sam is learning the bitter lesson of what it
means to be a money changer. The introduction of
American coin into Porto Rico has almost destroyed
the government of the islands.
The return of the refugees from China and their
story of barbarous warfare has brought to our very
doors a realization of the terrible tragedy of the
Orient.
France is building for herself a treasury of American
ill-will. First it was our wines against whichyshe
discriminated, and now it is our fruits. It is within
the range of possibility that she will go further in her
antagonism to us and tiy next to inflict upon us her
manners. i : f\%
Democratic dodgers may declare for free silver in
Colorado and denounce it in New York, but they can
not evade the candidacy of Bryan himself. That is one
of the issues of the campaign that cannot be dodged.
Bryan will have to raise his calamity howl much
higher this year than he did in 1896 if he expectsto
have it heard above the \vhirr of the Wheels of in
dustry and the songs of a prosperous people.- -* V
It has been often said that about every acre in China
is a cemetery, and that seems to be about all the ad
vance guard, of civilization is finding ; there.
The German Emperor possesses in all
111 residences. â
The railway from Tientsin to Peking a
distance of about seventy miles, was the
first constructed In China,
The "cow treo" of Venezuela gives a
fluid resembling, tasting like and possess
ing a close chemical affinity to cow's
milk.
Endless leather belts, acting as moving
staircases, convey the patrons of the
large Parisian stores from one floor to an
other.
A New \ork druggist said recently that
according to his experience men use hair
dyes to a much greater extent than
women.
Mayor Colonna of Rome has begun a
crusade against expectoration. The sign
E vietato da sputare is now up in all tav
erns and tobacco shops.
A Cleveland Judge has decided that
"Any woman is justified in scolding her
husband if he comes home drunk and Ill
treats her and the children."
A prize has been offered by a German
society for tho best design for an electric
railway upon which trains can travel at

CULLED FOR
CALL READERS
FOREST INCENDIARISM.
OVER and over again The Call has warned the
holiday crowds that flock to the woods during
the summer season to beware of the danger of
starting those fires in the fields and the woods that
are so disastrous to the State. In these warnings it
has been assumed thnt the danger of such conflagra
tions was to be found almost wholly in the negligence
and carelessness of hunters, campers and others wan
dering in the woods and meadows, but it now appears
there are some people sufficiently vicious and reckless
to deliberately start fires with the intention of de
stroying crops, pastures and forests. *
In the reports from Los Angeles concerning the
fires in the mountains in that section -of the State it
was said: "Not to carelessness or accident alone is
attributed the widespread havoc wrought by the fire
in the Sa'n Gabriel forest reserve. To the act of
criminal negligence, to which, with apparent justice,
is ascribed the origin of the conflagration that has
been raging in the mountains north of Sierra Madre
for nearly two weeks, now is added the crime of in
cendiarism, according to late reports from the scene
of the fire in the Little Santa Anita canyon. If these
reports be true, the fire which broke out afresh in
the Little Santa Anita Wednesday afternoon was de
liberately set in three different places by some mis
creant." *
There is hardly any kind of malicious mischief a ras
cal can commit with such comparative safety from
detection as that of starting a fire in a field or in the
woods, consequently when a person is detected in
committing such an act the punishment should b?
about as severe as the law permits. Our forests are
being destroyed too rapidly by the hands of those who
at worst are only reckless or careless, and we cannot
admit any plea to be made in mitigation of the of
fense of the: man who deliberately starts such a fire.
The act is one of -wanton criminality and the cost to
the community may run up into millions of dollars.
Its punishment should therefore be as swift, as stern
and as sure as the*justice of the State can effect.

EDUCATED FILIPINO "SAVAGES."
THE yellow journals teem with reports concern
ing the "rebels" and "savages" of the Philip
pine Islands, but the truth is that these people
are neither "rebels" nor "savages."
They are not "rebels," because they have never
given their consent to the bargain by which they were
turned over to us by Spain, like so many sheep, for
the consideration of twenty million dollars.
Neither are they "savages," as a brief survey of the
history of education in the Philippines will show.
Only two years after the Hudson River was discov
ered, and nine years before- the Pilgrim Fathers
landed on Plymouth Rock, the Dominican Fathers
founded the College of Santo Tomas at Manila. In
1645 â about fifty years before the time when the good
people of Salem killed twenty old women and Indians
for witchcraft â the college of these benighted Philip
pine "savages" was erected into a university by Pope
Innocent X, and regular instruction was given by a
faculty of arts and a faculty of theology. In 1734 â
fifteen years before Benjamin Franklin founded the
University of Pennsylvania â a faculty of law was
added. In 1845â three years before gold was discov
ered in California, when the water-rat dived where
now run the Montgomery-street electric cars and
when the coyote howled on the site of San Jose â in
1845 the University of Manila had 581 students; in
1858 â ten years before the University of California
was founded â it had icoo. That is a pretty good rec
ord, in higher education, for "savages."
You cannot have a university without fairly good
primary and secondary schools leading up to it.
Even if we knew nothing about such education in the
Philippines, this " irrefutable, a priori proposition
would prove the existence of such schools. But we
have concrete testimony as to the existence of such
schools in the accounts published by Mallat (Paris,
1846), .Semper (Wurzburg, 1869), and by Maichs
(Paris. f887). According to these observers the
"Indians" (Tagales and Visayas) of the Philippine?
had alphabets of their own and could read and write
even before the arrival of the Spaniards. Blumen
tritt, the German ethnologist, who has made a close
study of the ethnic traits of these "Indians," says that
the number of those who attend the secondary schools
and the university is "relatively very large," and that
from them have come such politicians as Rizal, Del
Pilar and Ponce; such artists as Luna; ' such eth
nographers as Florentino and such linguists as Lak
tar, "who are all known outside of their own coun-
The Deutsche Rundschau for 1897 contains a
biographical notice of Jacobo Zobel de Zangroniz, a
Spanish-German who was born in Manila of wealthy
parents and received his university education in Ger
many. Returning to Manila, he became distinguished
as a litterateur and a scientist. His testimony as to
the character of the Filipino "savages" among whom
he lived is strangely at variance with that brayed out
by yellow imperialists who concoct foreign "news" at
home. "Two-thirds of the Tagales can read," he
says, "and about half of them can write. They are a
cheerful, peaceable people, are disposed to enjoyment,
and have an eye rather to pleasures and things that
are beautiful and attractive than to the useful and
profitable. * * * Art, especially music, is their
passion."
It is too much to c/pect of yellow journalists that
they should be able to write, but it may be presup
posedârashly, perhapsâ that they share with two
thirds of the Tagales the capacity to read. To those of
them who possess this accomplishment we commend
the perusal of the Precis upon which this article is
James Taylor Rogers continues to insist that he
should not be compelled to talk. It is possible that
even he thinks that his actions in connection with
probate affairs are far more eloquent than any words
he could speak. .
TEACHING CIVILIZATION.
THE Kaiser has been criticized for his gory pros
pectus of the work he intends his army shall do
for the cause of Christian civilization in Chim.
Take no prisoners and give no quarter is his terse
recipe for an exhibit of the superiority of Christian
civilization to barbarism.
Eut what he promises Russia seems to be already
performing. Germany and Russia, by their aggres
sions, their lawless seizure of territory, their violation
of every international right of China, ha%-e supplied
the provocation for r.n uprising which has made it
temporarily impossible for China to perform her in
ternational obligations. The assault on the Taku
forts and the sacking of Tientsin were acts of mili
tary aggression, based on reports of slaughter of the
foreign legations at Peking which have proved to
have been false.
The conduct of the Russian troops in those actions
at arms is now, for the first time, being made public
in detail.
The American missionaries who reached San Fran
cisco on the Logan were witnesses of the scenes
which they describe. Rev. Mr. Hayner, a Methodist
missionary, says the clash at Taku was caused by false
reports started by the Russians. When the Chinese
officers found themselves overpowered and capitulated
and marched out of the forts and offered their swords
in surrender, they were shot down by the Russians
"like a lot of dogs." Mr. Hayner says that in one
village the Russians drove the women and children
into the river, and when they attempted to come
ashore they were all shot and their bodies floated
away on the stream. He adds that the brutality of the
rr.inions of the Czar was astonishing in these days of
supposed civilization.
Mrs. Drew, wife of the Tientsin Commissioner of
Customs, says that when the foreigners vacated their
houses in that city after the surrender the residences
were looted, the Russians stealing everything they
could lay hands on, and they went around carrying
the heads of Chinese on their bayonets. '
It is gratifying that all of these refugees highly com
mend the coolness, humanity and good judgment of
Admiral Kempff. His abstention at Taku and refusal
tc believe the Russian report and follow the Cossack
aggression have made Americans the most popular
foreigners in China.
Indeed we may congratulate ourselves that Presi
dent McKinley, while standing firm for our interna
tional rights, has tempered firmness with the highest
fense of justice and the most intelligent appreciation
of the difficulties of the Chinese.
It is reported that this wise attitude has impressed
the Czar, who seems to see the hopelessness of justi
fying to the world a seizure of Chinese territory as
long as America's example stands in the way. It is
believed now that he is willing to join the United
State? in an agreement that the integrity of Chinese
territory shall be respected.
If this be true it is a conspicuous diplomatic triumph
for the United States. It will be in the interest cf
humanity, justice and the righteousness of the world
and will do more for civilization than a million sol
diers and a thousand battles won.
The great fear has been that out of the Chinese
situation would issue a prolonged war, first against
China and then by the jealous European powers
?gainst each other. If we succeed in drawing Russia,
England and Japan into line with the United States
r.nd get them in agreement to respect the territorial
rights of China, the war there will be of short dura
tion, and the apprehended after consequences will
r.ot take place. \

HORSE PULLS OWNER'S TOOTH
A horse performed the duties of a den
tist at Babylon, N, Y., the other day, and
in a most expert manner.
William J. Smith was at work on his
place with his horse, when the animal
became restless on account of the files and
switched its tail vigorously. To Smith's
utter astonishment the horse's tail became
caught in the stem of his pipe and yanked
it out, carrying an incisor with It.
The tooth was extracted as quickly.
Smith says, and with no more pain than
if the operation had been performed by a
dentist. The tooth was perfectly sound.

THE ZODIAC-W. R. City. The zodiac
Is an imaginary broad belt In the heavens,
extending about 8 degrees on each side
of the ecliptic, within -which the apparent
motion of the sun, moon and the most
conspicuous of the planets, being those
known to the ancients, are confined It is
when the moon is in that imaginary belt
that she is alluded to as being "in the
zodiac." ____lili!:^-

A MENACE OF THE SEAS.
7- HE collier Wellington, on her arrival at this
port on Sunday, reported speaking the tugs
Rescue and Ta'toosh sixteen miles northwest of
Cape Mendocino with a log raft in tow, and it was
added the raft appeared to be going to pieces and big
logs were floating out from it. Later reports arc to
the effect that the /logs are drifting all * along the
Mendocino coast. That, of course, means a serious
menace to shipping, for the logs floating, about the
seas and tossed by the waves imperil every vessel
across whoSe course they drift.
The evil results of the attempts to tow huge rafts
along the coast have long been known to all who have
any dealings with marine affairs. In times past, when
similar means were employed in transporting lumber
from Maine to New York, the dangers to shipping
along the Atlantic coast were so great nnd the dis
asters so numerous that it was found necessary to for
bid the practice. It is now high time for preventive
measures to be adopted on this coa3t. A bill to that
effect has been presented to Congress by Senator
Perkins, but no action has as yet been taken upon k.
New efforts will of course be made to bring about the
enactment of the measure at the coming session, but
in. the meantime it will be worth while to consider
whether something in the way of remedy cannot be
provided at home.
Could the Harbor Commissioners forbid V the tow
ing of these huge rafts into the bay, or refuse them
landing privileges, the profits of the business would
be at an end, and the business; itself would stop.
Nobody would tow a raft down the coast if he could
not bring it into the harbor. It would seem, there
fore, to be well worth while for the Commissioners
to see what can be achieved by their authority in the
matter, for Congressional action is slow, and in an
emergency of this kind every day of delay adds to
the dangers.
The profits derived from successful raft shipments
must be very large, for attempts to raft millions of feet
of lumber to the city are made every year, notwith
standing that in nine cases out of ten the rafts go to
pieces. It is said that half a raft landed in San Fran
cisco will pay a dividend. The business is seemingly
something like a gamble, in which the chances
are against the adventurer, but a big reward awaits
the one who makes the trip successfully. The sup
pression of the attempts in that direction would,
therefore, be no injury to the legitimate lumber trade,
while it would be of great benefit to all kinds of ship
ping.
> The subject is one the merchants of the city should
at once investigate for the purpose of devising means
of checking the evil in advance of the action of Con
gress. If it be found that nothing can be done by
local authorities, then appeal should be made at once
to the officials at Washington. It is quite possible
that something could be effected in the way of rem
edy by the Federal officers under the general powers
given them to regulate coast trade. Nothing will be
lost, at any .rate, by an agitation of the subject. The
evil has been totally suppressed, we believe, on the
Atlantic coast, and there is no reason .why a similar
policy should not be enforced here.

v FIGURES REGARDING THE

SIZE OF FAMILIES
The average size of
families will be shown
to have decreased, but
with an increase In the
number of persons liv
ing in a dwelling, ow
ing to tho number of
larare apartment houses
built.
The most densely
settled area In the

shown to be Included
in the two square de
prees between latitudes
40 and 41 and longitudes
73 and 73, comprising

Williamsburg and Long

tal population of about
4,000,000.
DECREASE SHOWN IN

POLITICAL HYPOCRISY.
\A R. HEARSTS Journal, one of the heavenly
/ \ twins in journalislh that were put into the
>' California platform, is making good Senator
Hill's cynical indorsement of the Kansas City plat
form. He said that one part of it could be advocated
in one section of the country and other parts in other
sections.
The free silver declaration is good in Colorado and
the intermountain States, but is offensive in the East.
Therefore Hearst's Journal proceeds to give a lethal
dose of editorial to free silver. He declares that it is
not in present politics, but was put into the platform
out of consideration for the personal feelings of
Colonel Bryan, though the convention was against it
by a two-thirds majority. Therefore, the Journal
says, in no contingency can the Democratic party be
expected to legislate for the restoration of silver, and
that the silver issue is dead and out of politics.
To this the Stockton Mail makes reply that "if four
years ago it had been printed as a forecast of Demo
cratic sentiment in 1000 it would have been hooted by
the whole party, save the handful of gold men. Is it.
therefore, the true sentiment of the party to-day? If
it is, what a lesson it conveys! It presents the Demo
crats in the position of taking the medicine prepared
for them by the Republicans and saying it is good,
i Even the Republicans four years ago would not have
i dared to treat the silver question as a dead issue.
Now the only thing that has happened to alter the
situation has been brought about by the agency of the
Republican party. It is the very thing which the
Democrats and scores of learned publicists through
out the country had warned the people against. They
declared that from the bold position of holding all
obligations of the Government payable in gold, no
matter what the language of the contract might be.
to the enactment of a law affirming the gold dollar to
be the unit of value, was a step so easy that it would
surely be taken. The results following such a con
tiaction of the currency were pictured as of the direst
character. At-iast it has happened, and we find the
country concurring in it as a whipped child, simply
because it is an accomplished fact. The efforts of the
New York Journal to make it appear that the gold
standard vtrill in no contingency be in danger from the
party which has resolved in favor of free silver con
stitutes the great anomaly of the campaign to date.
Gold votes cannot be coaxed over by any such
friendly Indian pretense, whereas there are a lot of
silver votes and good, hard-headed Democratic votes
that would throw themselves to anything to beat a
party so pusillanimous, treacherous and contemptible
as to seek support from the enemy by promising to be
grood to it."
There you have it. The Janus face is glowering
upon the country. Democrac3' is one thing in New-
York and quite another thing in California. But the
California convention has specifically indorsed the
Journal as a great exponent of Democracy, and it ex
pounds to the effect that no contingency can arise in
which the party will overthrow the gold standard es
tablished by the Republicans. Yet the daily papers
are talking of Chinese duplicity as an article superior
to any of which the Caucasian is capable!
based. They will find it in volume I, page 968, of the
last report of the United States Commissioner of
Education.

astounding artistic value of the collection [
may be derived from the statement that
It contains 46 paintings by Murillo, 14 by
Zurbaran. oS by Rlbera. 64 by Velasquez.
55 by Tenters. Cfi by Rubens. 10 by Raph
ael, 20 by Poussin, 66 by Luca Giordano,
22 by Van Dyck, 34 by Breughel. 10 by
Claude Lorraine, 16 by Guido Renl, 43 by
Titian, 64 by Tintoretto and 25 by Paul
Veronese.
This magnificent collection of paintings
Is very well housed and advantageously |
displayed in the Prado. and the general i
good condition of the canvases testifies to Â¦
both the intelligent care of the director
and the Cry, pure air of Madrid. The
catalogue, edited by the eminent writer.
Don Pedro de Madrizo. Is well made, and
is issued in both Spanish and French. The I
gallery is open, free to the public on Sun- |
day and on weekdays there is a small ad- |
mlttanee fee, equivalent to a dime. From
the Puerta del Sol (the great square in
the center of Madrid) the visitor ap
proaches the museum through the Car
rera de San Geronimo, passing the palace
of the Cortes and a fine monument to Cer
vantes on the way.
The museum contains two main picture
galleries, each about 130 by 33 feet in di
mensions, wall lighted from the top, and
devoted to the Spanish school. The num
erous side galleries 'opening to right and
left are lighted from the sides, therefore
the light is fluctuating and on cloudy days
rather scanty. These rooms are devoted
to the Italian. Dutch, French and Flem
ish schools, respectively. Half way down
the great central galleries a door opens
into the so-ealied salon of Queen Isa
bel, a room hung with the most popular
and famous paintings in the collection,
chosen from all schools and forming a
group of about 100 works, brought togeth
er on the same plan as that of the balon
Carre of the Louvre and the Tribune of
Florence. This gallery Is oval, and Is
lighted from overheard. Were the actual
and supposed masterpieces here assem
bled to be weeded out to-day, there would
be some radical changes in the arrange
ment, no doubt; however, the room Is re
markably rich in good things as It Jss. To
name only a few of the most celebrated
and valuable works In it, there are Ra
phael's "Virgin of the Fish." "Holy Fam
ily of the Lamb," "Virgin of the Row,"
"Holy Family of the Lizard" and "Por
trait of a Cardinal"; Titian's "Tribute to
the Goddess of Love," "Venus and
Adonis," "Bacchanal" and portraits of
Charles V and Philip II; Tintoretto's
"Portrait of a Young Man." "Sebastian
Venieri." etc.; Velasouez'jf "Drinkers,"
"Mercury and Argus." "Portrait of a
Sculptor." "Court Jester." "Forge of \ ul ;
can,'' "St. Anthony's Visit to St. Paul *
"Don Fernando of Austria" and 'Portrait
of a Man," with many choice examples
of Veronese. Rubens. \ an Dyck, Holbein.
Claude. Poussln. Ribera. Moro. Murillo.
Coiresrgio, Giorglone, IMirer. Cano. Rem
brandt: Fra Angelico. Giulio Romano.
Guldo. Van Dyck. Lunl and Guerdno. In
fact, there are few rooms in the world
holding a more striking group of interna-
But the one supreme and sufficient
glory of the Madrid Museum Is not the
heterogeneous gathering of star can
vases in the salon of Queen Isabel
it is the possession of the only great ana
representative collection of the wor ks of
Velasquez, the prince of Spanish painters,
and one of the most extraordinary lum
inaries of the world of art since painting
was invented. This distinction alone
would be enough to set the Madrid Mu
seum apart upon a pinnacle of ita own.
Without traveling to Madrid no one can
really know Velasquez. He stands espe
cially for the somewhat indefinable but
nevertheless very real spirit of aristoc
racv. which was to some extent exempli
fied" in the work of Van Dyck though In
a lfss deep way. The fact of his being
a court painter does not necessarily en
ter into the causes of his mental attitude
toward mankind. Velasquez was more
aristocratic himself than were his noblest
fitters. He was the gentleman of gentle
men In art, and he ennobled everything
that ho tcucbed. He has reserve power:
there is a touch of perfection In his re
finement that makes all pretense seem
Ineffably mean; never has there been
Â«uch genuineness, such perfect poise, such
unasÂ«:umed distinction of manner, such un
conscious authority and inborn elegance.
The first thought of most persons, on see
ing a portrait by Velasquez for the first
time Is: "How very simple and easy! Is
thatVll there is of this wonderful artist?"
There is a feeling akin to disappointment:
one was possibly expecting: something
d?zzlingr. unusual, phenomenal. But no:
there is nothing in Velasquez that !s sen
sational at all: his scheme of color is usu
ally very quiet, based on pray and brown
tones without great brilliance, though
soberly rich and deep: striving for effect
is utterly foreign to him: he was Just sim
ply the most natural of painters.
The most remarkable pictures by Velas
quez are "Las Hilanderas" ("The Tar*?s
try Weavers"). "Las Meninas" ("The
Maids of Honor"). "Las Lanzas" ("The
Surrender of Breda"), an equestrian por
trait of the Duke of Ollvarez. the various
portraits of King Philip IV and of his
children, the Princess Mary Margaret and
Prince Balthasar Charles, with the eight
pieces hung in the salon of Queen Isa
bel, which have been mentioned above.
But it must be remembered that all of
Velasquez's sixty-four pictures in Madrid
are remarkable, Â¦ and that all the other
museums in the world cannot show so
many, to say nothing of quality. Nothing
that Velasquez did was trivial or un
worthy, though it would be too much to
expect that all of his productions should
exhibit equal merits.
Perhaps there does not exist a more per
fect specimen of painting than "Las Me-
were his early mythological Â»ubjects fully
expressive of hi3 true personality. Some of
his landscapes are vastly admired by good
judges of art, but he used landscape cus
tomarily merely as a background for his
portraits. Among the American painters
who have worshiped fervently at the
shrine of the master are John S. Sargent.
James McN. Whistler, William M. Chase
and Frederick P. Vinton. The purity and
nobility of his style make him an admira
ble examplar for painters. Hla Influence*
is wholesome and elevating. He had every
negative merit possible â no tricks of tha
brush, no commonplaces, no affectation.
no artificiality, no self-consciousness. A
profound and innate integrity of sentiment,
an utter and unsought Individuality and
originality, and a complete spontaneity
and naturalness set his works apart. Their
material beauty and nobility is apparent
to all, but their highest and deepest qual
ity Is spiritual and mental, the perfectest
pictorial expression of a rare and lofty
soul.

WATER FRONT NOTES.
Miss Becbe Bean, who came back on
the Sherman, is well known here. She has
been nearly prostrated with dysentery,
but is now recovering, as a friend of the
soldiers she was known all over the Pre
sidio, and when the City of Para sailed
for Manila with her favorite regiment she
dressed in male attire and sailed with
them as one of the cabin boys. Since then
she has been among the men at all times
and comes home borne down by sickness,
but laden with "news."
Private Dave Boltz will probably end
his days on Alcatraz. He Is sentenced to
forty years' imprisonment. While under
arms Boltz deserted to the enemy and
now there is no name more execrated
among the troops in the Philippines than
his.

The army transport Sherman came over
from quarantine yesterday and docked at
Folsom 2. She made a record run of twen
ty-two days from Manila and Chief En
gineer Kingsley Is jubilant in conse
quence. There was never a hitch through
out the journey and the machinery per
formed like clockwork.

RECENTLY ARRIVED VESSEL THAT IS THE TAIK OF THE WATER FRONT.
THE four-maeted Americana that ar- |
rived from the nitrate ports via
Honolulu late Monday night is the
most peculiarly rigged schooner that
ever came inrST* this port. No one is
willing to father the Invention, but
judging from the schooner's perform
ances the rig is not a success. The
Americana was 159 days coming from
Caleta Buena, and at one time 50 per cent
reinsurance was paid on her. She put
into Honolulu, however, and was then 61
days coming from that port to San Fran
cisco. This Is the record slow passage
from the nitrate ports to San Francisco.
The Americana carries a jib topsall,-Â»*rn
inward and an outward Jib, a fore gaff
topsail, a fore and aft foresail, a square
foresail, a main topgallant sail, main top
sail and fore and aft mainsail, mizzen and
gaff topsail and spanker and gaff topsail.
In spite of all this spread of canvas the
best the Americana could do was about
100 knots a day. She was very foul, how
ever, having been for a long time in the
water without being drydocked.
Captain Berry got himself into trouble
with the quarantine officer. He had not
been In San Francisco for nearly five
years, and not knowing of changes in the
quarantine laws sailed his vessel in and
anchored off Harrison-street wharf. Yes
terday Dr. Kinyoun ordered the vessel
back to an anchorage off Black Point,
There he visited her and then Captain
Perry was allo'wed to dock his vessel at
Union rtreet.
ANOTHER SCHOONER IN TROUBLE.
The Carrier Dove from New Ycrk for
Seattle put in here In distress late Mon
day night. Her rudder was damaged and
the vessel was leaking, so the captain de
cided to make San Francisco. This was
all the mere necessary as the crew was
almost down to starvation diet. For three
weeks there had been nothing to eat but
beans. There was no canned goods and
no bread, but luckily plenty of water.
"Water and be;\ns therefore were the sta
ples on the Carrier Dove for three weeks.
Some of the men on the little schooner
teH wild stories. A sea serpent swallow
ed half the loRline in the straits of Ma
gellan, a witch doctor drove the Bhlp's
dog into spasms, the members of the Lon
don Missionary Society in Patagonia have
turned pirates and are looting passing
vessels, an ex-missionary has a mania for
murdering the natives and a thousand
notches on the barrel of his gun is the
record of the number of natives murdered
in cold blood. These and similar blood
curdling stories the sailors tell of their
sojourn In the "land of flre."
The Carrier Dove was intended for ser
vice between Fuget Sound ports, but it is
doubtful if she will ever reach there. It
will cost considerable to repair .her and
the chances are she will be sold to the
highest bidder. When the schooner was
at Punta Arenas two of the crew desert
ed and men to take their places were
picked up on the beach. On arrival here
Crew of Schooner Carrier Dove Says Lo % ndo.n Mis
sionaries in Patagonia Have Turned Pirates
and Are Looting Passing Vessels.

The Royal Museum of the Prado In Ma
drid contains the most remarkable col
lection of pictorial masterpieces in the
world. It Is a collection which makes
no claim to completeness, and the primi
tive examples of the various European
schools are almost wholly wanting In it.
Again, although a certain master may be
fully represented by an unequaJed group
of his best works, another and equally
great artist of the school may not be rep
resented In the museum at all. This fault.
If It be one, is easily overlioked In view
of the marvelous strength of the collec
tion in certain special lines, which makes
It one of the most sacred of all meccas
for the artistic pilgrim. Thus, while It is
inferior to the Louvre in Paris as a his
toric assemblage of the world's greatest
paintings, it Is even more brilliant as a
collection of masterpieces, more or Ies3
unrelated and more or less unclassified.
The number of paintings in the Madrid
gallery exceeds 2200. and some idea of the
nlnas." which depicts Velasquez hlmsell In
his studio, palette in hand, in the act of
painting the portraits ct Philip IV and the
Queen, wnile in the foreground the young
Princess Marparet Mary ot Austria is be-
I Inp entertained by her maids of honor, too*
I cludlnj; two dwarfs. So exactly and sat
j isfactorlly docs this interior with figures
I counterfeit the actual appearances of
j things, so adorable is the combination of,
! power and modesty with which it is paint
ed, so charming are tht; quaint types of
the maids In the foreground, so beautiful
and harmonious is the color, and so pal
pitating with life is the scene that it may
well be called a typical and ideal speci
men of the preat Spaniard's art. It is al
most impossible to realize that thesa ara
the firures of people who have been In
their graves at least two centuries and a
| half.
Indeed. Velasquez, belonging to the sev
enteenth century, was essentially modern
in his spirit. He founded in his own time
no school; but his Influence is more pow
erful to-day amonjr painters, and particu
larly amonjr portrait painters, than that
of any old master. IIo devoted hl3 mature

NEW YORK. Aus. 7.â The recent disposition
to refrain from operating in Ftocks and to await

inforced to-day by the extreme heat, which
reached a level Inducing prostrations on the

occasional execution of oniÂ«*rs in a few stocks
where special causes were at work, the list was
alir.ort wholly neglected. During the early part
of the day some considerable declines were es
tablished, but when the room traders came to
close up their contracts the losses were in large
part wiped out. The most conspicuous Individ
ual movement wa3 In United States rubber,
which Jumped S*i from the early low point not
long before the clore. Punishment was visited
rs a result upon the shcrt interest which rushed
to cover, making some enow of animation in
stocks. Northern Pacific's larce increase In
earnings for the fourth week In July brought
realizing and the grangers were heavy on the
weather bureau's weekly crop report, showing
need of rain in the corn belt. The steel stocks
sagged on the further Â»-Â»ductlon in the prices ot
iron warrants. Rome of the recently inactive
stocks showed wide declines, including Chl
capo Great Western, Lake Erie & 'Western.
Iowa Central and the local traction stocks.

beginning of the told export movement. En-
Rapements amounted to only about $2,500,000,
whereas the miniates yesterday pointed to
$4,000,000 and upward. Reports from London
were that the Bank of England was now giv
ing special facilities to attract gold from New
York. Otherwise it Is not probable that gold
would have gone out at to-day's figure for
sterling. The rate for demand sterling fell
back a fraction aÂ« a result of sales of bills
against irold exports. It is announced, however,
that more (fold will go out Thursday. To-day's
shipments are supposed to have l<eÂ«>n to cover
the required deposit of 5 r#r cent accompany
ing applications for war loans.

ft") lysue for the United States therefore In
sures American bidders about SO per cent of
their applications. Future payments are re
quired on August 15 of 20 per cent, on Septem
ber 14 of 2S per cent, on October 15 of 23 per
cent and r.n November 15 of 23 per cent, of the
bonds Issued at 98. This would make It feasible
to distribute the remittances over a consider
able period, but there is also a provision that
the installments may be paid in full on or
after August 15, under a discount at the rate
of 3 per cr.L No information can be obtained
as to how remittances will be made and this
contuses the estimates ae to the future effect
on the money market. Gold continues to ac
cumulate in New York on payments by the sub
treasury to the banks of drafts on account of
sold deposited at Pacific points. The banks

CHICAGO. Aug. 7.â At the opening in wheat
and a few minutes after an excitedly lively
business was done compared with the almost
utter stagnation of recent sessions. Early
quotations from Liverpool furnished a big sur
prlce to local traders, showing an advance in
that market of lUÂ©l%d. The advance was said
to be due to exceptionally bad weather all over
the United Kingdom, greatly interfering with
harvesting operations. Opening quotations In
September ranged from about %c to a full cent
over yesterday's closing prices, the range bn-
Ing from 764c to 764c. But there the advance
stopped and it took but a few minutes of the
heavy realizing which started at once to force
prices back quite materially. Offerings for
some time wer* heavy, long wheat coming out
in large quantities, principally from local hold
ers, though houses with foreign connection*
were also prominent In the selling. There were
apparently plenty of buying orders In the mar
ket at 7Cc and under and the market was noi
often allowed to go below that figure up to
the noon hour, the lowest point touched up to
that time being 75T(.c. After noon the market,
which had gradually become extremely dull,
became heavy also. The crowd was evidently
long and the market declined to 75%c, mostly
from its own weight. Shortly before the close
liberal export sales gave a better tone to thi
market, September* advancing to 7Â«Vic bid,
where it closed.
Corn was dull for the most part, but firm at
all times. September closed %@"ic higher at
3S4fc3SHe.
Oats were quiet, but steady. September
closed >;c higher at 214@22c.
Provisions were steady, with a fair general
trade, with packers the best buyers. There
was some outside demand, presumably in
fluenced by the Government demands for meats.
There were no new disturbing elements as re
gards the yellow fever outbreak. At the clois
September pork was 24c higher; lard and ribs
each 24c higher.
The leadlnr futures ranged as follows:

NEW YORK, Aug. 7.â The market for cot
ton futures started barely steady in tone, with
prices 1 to 3 points higher on near months
and 2 to 4 points lower on far months. Bear
supremacy soon asserted itself after the call
and under fairly active selling for both ac
counts prices crumbled sharply. Against $7 93
in the morning October advanced to js 17 before
a reaction Â«et In. January advanced from
$7 85 to $8 05. At the best figures of the day
the market showed a net rise of 11 to 14
points. The market finally steadied, with
prices 5 to 12 points higher.

The East is getting impatient for new Prunes
and Raisins and is sending a good many tele
grams out here for quotations, which, however,
they are unable to get. The quotations for
Prunes may be expected any day now, though
it is possible they may still be delayed a week
yet. Nobody seems to have any idea as to what
the opening base price will be.
The fruit market as a rule Is firm and slow
ly tending upward. Apricots are leading, as
choice stock is scarce and the East is looking
for It. With the, possible exception of Apples
there is no really weak fruit on the list.
Butter, Eggs and Cheese continue quiet at
about the same quotations. All are in ample
supply, and Cheese is in especially large stock,
with more sellers than buyers. The demand
for packed Butter and cold storage Eggs is
not yet heavy, though some sales are being
made.

NEWS MATTERS ANENT
STATE UNIVERSITY
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
1118 Broadway, Aug. 7.
The sequel of a runaway match was
seen in Judge Hall's court to-day when
Celia Paul was prranted a divorce from
James Paul on the ground of desertion.
And in tho story of her years of waiting
was the evidence of a woman's devotion.
In 1SS3 James Paul, who was the pro
prietor of a tailoring 1 ship in San Fran
cisco, wooed and won the woman who
has just been legally separated from him.
As the girl's parents objected to Ihe
match an elopement followed and tho
lovers were married In San Rafael. When
parental forgiveness was forthcoming
they went to live at the home of the
bride.
One evening in February, 1SS4. Paul did
not return home from nis business. In
vestigation showed that he had sold out
his shop and departed, carefully conceal
ing his tracks. Mrs. Paul did not, how
ever, lose faith in her spouse. -During the
sixteen years since his abrupt disappear
ance she has waited patiently for his re
turn. Only twice has she heard rumor*
of him. Once he was said to be in jail
and now he Is supposed to be in New
York.
At last Mrs. Paul concluded that pa
tience was becoming a -fault and the di
vorce granted by Judge Hall was the, out
come of her change of mind. The custody
of the only child is given to the mother.

OAKIiAND, Aug. 7. â Mrs. M. E.

Judge Lawlor's Final Evening Ses
sion in Department 11 Will
Close at Midnight!
Aliens who desire to be naturalized in
time to vote at the November election will
be given a last chance to-night. At mid
night the time limit will expire and natur
alization after that time will not carry
with It the right to vote on- November 6
Superior Judge William P. Lawlor wlli
sit in Department 11 at the City Hall to
night after 7 o'clock to hear applications
for naturalization, for the accommodation
of those who may find it impossible to at
tend the regular sessions of the court to
day.
At the session of Judge Lawlor's court
last night about 150 applications for nat
uralization were heard. A rush of appli
cants Is expected at the final sitting to
night. -'-;â¢Â¦ . â¢ ...
LAST CHANCE GIVEN TO
BECOME NATURALIZED
COLLAPSE OF INSECURE
WALLS CAUSES PANIC
.Rickety, Structure Trembles, but For
tunately Does Not Completely
Collapse.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
111S Broadway, Aug.' 7.
The collapse of the partitions. In the old
Cavalry Hall building, at Twelfth and
"Washington streets, which is being remod
eled by a force of carpenters, was the
cause of a panic this afternoon among the
patrons of the stores on the ground floor
of the flimsy structure. Timbers were
hurled into the street, and an unknown
lady, walking on Washington street nar
rowly missed being struck, by a fourteen
inch beam as it fell from the second story
. Many thought the whole shell would
collapse, and places of safety were eagerly
sought.
About 3:30 this afternoon, while car
penters were sawing a fourteen-lnch tim
ber on the second floor, the wooden girder
snapped, and within a second nearly every
partition fell to the lloor with a crash
that threatened to plunge them through
on to the people In the stores beneath.
The rickety old structure trembled for
several minutes, and it was some time be
fore the carpenters resumed their work.
When the crash came the markets on
Twelfth . and Washington streets were
crowded with afternoon shoppers. Women
and children screamed and hurried for
the middle of the streets, and from the
Gas Kitchen restaurant, a score of cus
tomers, cooks and waiters rushed for the
open air.
Tho shock broke all the chandeliers in
the restaurant, besides demolishing a
quantity of crockery and glassware. Cav
alry Hall is a wooden building erected
many years ago by the late H. D. Bacon,
and it now belongs to his estate. It is
now being remodeled. There are walls
only one brick thick on two sides only,
and the work of the carpenters has great
ly weakened the old structure.

BY CHIEF OF POLICE
Commissioners Give Serg-eant Gleason
Instructions That Saloons Must
Be Closely Watched.
The Police Commissioners held a
lenstby meeting last evening. Patrol
tr.an Timothy J. Calnan, who left town
pome three week? ago under a cloud, was
formally dismissed from the force.
Policeman Edward J. Thompson, who
Flapped Sergeant Blank In the face Sun
day, was given a hearing. It developed
that the offense was committed in a tem
porary fit of anper. the policeman being
f';iKht!y under the Influence of liquor at
the time. The sergoants over Thompson
testified to his previous exemplary char
ucter and represented him as a brave,
gentlemanly and efficient offlcer. Thomp
son testified that he had taken a few
drinks and that his head was troubling
him at the time he struck the sergeant.
The Commissioners voted to take the mat
ter under advieement for one week.
Commissioner Mahoney came in affer
the hearing and when the board finished
Its regular business It took up the Thomp-
F"n case again, but failed to reach any
agreement.

r'-porting for duty a half hour late. His
excuse was that he had forgotten his star
and returned home for It after Dearly
reaching the station.

The board took no action on the matter.

ROGERS SENT TO
SERVE OUT HIS
FULL FIVE DAYS
After the manner of the King of France
and his famous march up the hill and
down again, James Taylor Rogers went
from Jail to the Supreme Court and from
the Supreme Court to Jail again. His pe
titioji for a writ of habeas corpus was
"discharged" yesterday and he was re
manded to the custody of tho Sheriff.
The petition was heard before Justices
Beatty. Harrison. Henshaw, Temple and
Van Dyke, the opposing counsel stipulat
ing after the hearing was over that thÂ«
absent Justices. Garoutte and Harrison,
nhould be allowed to participate in the
decision. It looked at lirst, when the
court retired to deliberate, that there
would be no decision except on the ques
tion of allowing bail, for which Mr. Hen
ley had asked, but Judge Garoutte came
in while they were discussing the matter
and with him a decision on the principal
issue was reached. The opinion was writ
ten by, Justice Henshaw, Justices Harri
eon, Garoutte and Van Dyke concurring.
So far as the decision Is concerned, it
throws but little light ot the case and
only paves the way for another proceed
ing in contempt and another appeal. There
were eleven questions propounded which
Rogers refused to answer. If any one of
these were rele\-ant and pertinent and
capable of being answered without tend
ing to incriminate the witness or degrade
his character, then in refusing to re
ply the witness was clearly in contempt.
Then, looking over the questions, the
court found this one: "Did John M. Chre
tien Inform you that John Sullivan was
not the legal heir of Joseph Sullivan, de
ceased?" This question, the court held,
was plainly pertinent and relevant, and
It Is apparent on the face of It that no
answer, either "yea" or "no," could pos-
Mbly incriminate or degrade the witness.
Therefore he was in contempt. Therefore
he must go back to Jail to serve out his
Hve days.
This completes the circle and Rogers
is Just where he started, except that in
trying to stand straight and avoid say
ing too much he has fallen backward and
landed in Jail. The principal issues in the
case have not been touched upon at all,
and when Rogers comes out of jail he will
be asked to answer the questions again.
He will undoubtedly answer the one pass
ed upon by the Supreme Court, and then
he may stick on the others if he pleases.
When he does the questions will go to
the Supreme Court again, where they
may be ruled upon.
When the proceedings opened yesterday
afternoon. Judge Heatty narrowed the in
quiry from the start to the validity of the
order of the Superior Court directing Rog
ers to answer the questions propounded.
If the order was invalid, he said, there
was no question but that Rogers had dis
obeyed it. Deuprey then objected to any
matters other than those Inclosed in the
last proceeding in court being made a
part of the case at bar, but Henley was
allowed a little more latitude.
He argued that tht\ affidavit setting
forth the refusal to answer and the con
sequent contempt, must show not only
the questions asKed but also that they
were relevant and material to the Issue.
AH In the affidavit that went In support
of their materiality, he said, was the
mere allegation that they were relevant
and material.
Deuprey then spoke briefly for the re-
Fpondents. He said it was a plain case of
a witness refusing to answer questions
after having been ordered to do so by the
court. The questions asked, he said, had
been decided by the Superior Court to be
relevant and material, and It had been
further ascertained by the court that they
did not tend to degrade the witness nor to
Incriminate him. He is not tho Judge of
the tendency of his answers, argued Deu
prey: it is fertile court to Judge of the
consequence. Tney were only before the
court to answer one questionâ had the
court exceeded Its Jurisdiction In making
tho order? If on the face of the commit
ment the court found that the lower court
had not exceeded Its Jurisdiction, then.
Deuprev claimed, the petitioner had no
standing before the Supreme Court.
The question of bail was then taken up.
It appeared to be a simple thing that
Henley asked when he requested that his
client be released on bail pending a de
cision In the case, but it presented an
other knotty problem and the court with
drew to weptle with it. It is the con
tention of some of the Supreme Court
Justices that in matters of habeas corpus
the Supreme Court has power only either
to discharge or remand the prisoner.
of the corporation has been due to hl3
efforts.

Was Hope for Rogers.

ers would go free after the length of time
the court was spending in deliberation,
leading tho waiting onVs to believe the

was gathered from the remarks of the
