1 The action of the Supreme Court may
not have ended with the remanding of
I Bogers. however. It is the opinion of
lawyers that the court may take Judicial
! notice of the grounds for Rogers' refusal
j to answer and disbar him by an order
I from the bench. Since the first ordeal be
j fore tho Grand Jury Bogers has taken no
' pains to conceal the fact that he fears he
will be convicted of a felony should he
! answer. Conviction of a felony is good
| cause for the disbarment of any attorney
! so convicted, and it is generally believed
j the Supreme Court can and will take this
; view of it. In that event no motion wili
\ be n<*essary on the part of the Bar As
i soclatlon or any other organization or per
; son. The affidavit of Rogers setting forth
: his fears under oath is evidence enough
I that he has been gtiilty nf something
! serious enough to make his disbarment
i necessary for the health of the legal pro
j fesslon. And that affidavit is on file in
the Supreme Court.

IGNACIO MARTINETTI, THE DANCER, WHO COMES TO AT
TEND THE BOHEMIAN JINKS.
T7GXACIO MARTINETTI, the original
Zou-Zou In "Trilby," arrived recently
j| in this city from New York. lie comes
to attend the midsummer Jinks of the
Bohemian Club, which takes place fn the
redwoods near Guernevllle the latter part
of this week.
Martlr.ctti Is a popular member of the
club and is a universal favorite. His cre
ation of the erratic Frenchman in "Tril
by" in the first production of the play
made him one of the foremost farcÂ«
comedians of America, and. In fact, of the
world. He is a native of this city, of
French parents, and has the quick wit
and the gallant manners of the true Pa
risian. His appearance at the Jinka will
no doubt go a long way toward making
the annual outing of tne members of the
Bohemian Club a {Treat success. Martl
nettl has been engaged and will appear
next week with the Frawley company at
the Grand Opera-house in the production
of "Trilby." in his old part of Zou-Zou.
For the past two years the popular
comedian hns bren steadily engaged In
Xev- York with May Irwin and has had
till his parts specially written for him.
He has a host of friends In this city who
will be glad tr> know he Is here for a
short trip, and he will no doubt be royally
entertained by them.

band. From the kitchen window of her j
home near the foot of Post street she saw j
a man sailing a small boat off the bay I
shore. Whilo ?he was looking the boat !
capsized, throwing the man into the wa- j
ter. He managed to grab tho keel. With j
one hand he held on and with tho othci j
waved frantically to attract attention. |
Mrs. Cavanaugh sent her S-year-old son j
Harry to tell her husband, who was at j
work on a scaffolding, of the accident
Letting himself to the ground as soon
as ho could, Cavanaugh ran to a skiff
belonging to him that Â¦was tied to the
shore. Hy the time the rope was untied
and he had sculled out to the struggling
man considerable time had elapsed. Tne
man lost his hold after becoming ex
hausted and went down. Cavanaush
thought he would reach him before he
â¢ ?
P. F. CAVANAUGH, THE HEJIO OF A SENSATIONAL RESCUE.
went down the second time. But the wind
was blowing hard, making It difficult to
manage the skiff with one oar. The res
cuer saw that he must act quicklv if
he would save the man. Without taking
time to remove any of his clothing he
jumped overboard and swimming to him
caught hold of his shirt collar.
The strupKles of the water-logged man
threatened to carry his rescuer down to
a watery grave with him. He grasped
Cavanangh i arms bo tightly as to make
him almost powerless, to swim. The
strons wind had blown the skiff away,
leaving nothing for C.ivanaugh to do but
make for the shore. Fortunately for the
men the tide was on the ebb. which as
sisted materially. Upon finally reaching
land CavanauRh fell, exhausted, with his
burden.
Rescuer and rescued were revived by"
stimu!nnt3 furnished by resident* who
had been attracted to the seem* Ty tho
accident. The rescued man ffave his
name as Max Johnson. He said he lived
in Fruitvale and had set out for a pleas
ure sail. The boat hnd been capsized by a
sudden gust of wind. Not being a'swim
mer, he was unable to right his boat or
to pet ashore. The boat was recovered
some distance from the point where it
capsized.
"*or a few minutes my life wasn't
worth the flip of a co!n."said Cavanauerh
to-day. He still showed the effects of his
battle with waves and wind.
CREATOR OF ZOU-ZOU
GUEST OF BOHEMIANS
INDIANAPOLIS. Aug. 7.â The special
train bringing Mr. Bryan and Mr. Steven
son to this city, where they are to-morrow
to be officially notified of their respective
nominations by the Democratic party for
the Presidency and the Vice Presidency,
reached here at 7:20 p.m. to-day.
The run from Chicago was delayed
somewhat by the demonstrations of
crowds which gathered along tho route.
The train was in charge of the County
Democracy of Chicaco, and it was com
posed of twelve coaches, all of them filled
to the brim with Democratic workers.
Messrs. Bryan and Stevenson, their fam
ilies, their friends and supporters, occu
pied the rear coach of the train. Chair
man Jones, of the National Committee,
and his wife, were in attendance, as were
(Jovernor and Mrs. Thomas, Secretary
Walsh and Sergeant at Arms Martin of
the National Committee, and many of the
leading members of that organization.
Samuel Alschulcr, Democratic candidate
for Governor of Illinois; Mayor Harrison
of Chicago and Webster D*vvis, lato As
sistant Secretary of the Interior under the
present administration, were among the
distinguished guests.
The afternoon was devoted largely to
conferences between the leaders of the
party, who were brought together for the
tirst time since the Kansas City conven
tion. Senator Jones expressed himself as
especially pleased with the outlook in
New York. Mr. Davis participated freely
in the conversations and announced his
intention to speak during the campaign
in several States of the Union.
Mayor Harrison introduced Mr. Bryan,
who said :
I am a very cautious man, and -while I have
seen It stated in the papers that I was nomi
nated for the Presidency I know that you can
not always trust what you see in the papers
(laughter) and I am not going to take It for
granted that I have been nominated until I am
officially notified of it down at Indianapolis.
I don't want to make a speech as the Presi
dential candidate until I am sure I am nomi
nated. (Laughter nnd great applause.) Mr.
Stevenson, the nominee for the Vice Presi
dency, Is along, and Governor Thomas of Colo
rado, who Is to notify him of his nomination,
ig on the train, and I believe has privately in
formed him that he has been nominated. There
fore he has more reason for making a speech
than I have, and I take pleasure in presenting
to you your candidate for Vice President, who
Is, I hope and believe, to be the next Vice
President of the United Staotes, Hon. Adlal
E. Stevenson. (Applause.)
The circumstance that the train was an
hour behind schedule time In reaching
Indianapolis did not seem to dampen the
interest of the great crowd which waited.
It was with much difficulty that the party
reached their carriages. When Mr. Bry
an's well known tigure was recognized In
the marching column a great shout was
sent up and cheers followed him con
stantly until he arrived at the Grand Ho
tel. The streets were lined so thickly
with people that it was with difficulty that
progress could be made, and it was 8 p. m.
beforo the travelers sat down to dinner.
After dinner Mr. Bryan and Mr. Steven
son wore given a general reception at the
Grand Hotel. They shook hands with a
very large number of people and chatted
pleasantly for a few moments with many
of them. Mrs. Bryan and Mrs. Stevenson
were entertained by a committee of ladles.

NEW OCEANIC 'STEAMER
LAUNCHED BY THE CRAMPS
PHILADELPHIA. Aug. 7.â The steam
ship Sonoma, built for the Oceanic Steam
ship Company, was launched at Cramps
ship yards at 10:33 a. m. to-day.
The Sonoma will engage In the trans
pacific trade between San Francisco and
Australia. With the exception of the St.
Louis and the St. Paul she Is the largest
merchantman ever built on the Delaware
River. The principal dimensions of the
vessel are: length between perpendicu
lars. 400 feet; beam, 50 feet: load draft 24
feet; displacement, 9700 tons. The con
tract speed is seventeen knots and the
ship Is to accommodate 400 passengers.
Enthusiastically Greeted by
a Large Crowd of
People.

Special Dicpatch to The Call.
DENVER, Aug. 7.â A special to the
News from Hugo, Colo., says; Fred Mer
rick was arrested in Elbert County to-day
and brought to Hugo at 4 o'clock on sus
picion of being one of the Union Pacific
train robbers. A posse Is after another
man thought to be Merrick's partner and
is expected to return some time to-night.

Stricken by Paralysis.
VENTURA. Aug. 7.â John Thomas, vice
president of the Temescal Oil Company,
was stricken with paralysis last nisht.
He is an important witness in the suit
before the Superior Court brought by the
Temescal Company against Juan Sequeda
et al. to quiet title to valuable oil prop
erty in the Piru district. Mr. Thomas
was rendered speechless. He was taken
to Los Angeles, this morning.

Miss Gertrude Moinin Alleges That
1 Dentist Cory Broke Her Jaw
in Pulling a Tooth.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
FRESNO, Aug. 7.â An Interesting dam
age suit was filed In the Superior Court
to-day. In which Miss Gertrude Moinin
Is the plaintiff and Dentist B. B. Cory of
this city defendant. Miss Moinin asks for
$20,000 as compensation for Injuries re
ceived by her while undergoing treatment
by Cory- The complaint sets out that on
October 15, 1S99, the plaintiff visited the
defendant for the purpose of having a
tooth extracted. The operation resulted,
it Is alleged. In the breaking and disloca
tion of the jaw. on account of which she
has since found it necessary to submit to
many operations, and that she Is perma
nently Injured, though she was a strong
and robust woman before. Up to the pres
ent time, It is alleged, she has paid out
$161 op account of the injuries received,
and sne asks for this sum in addition to
the damages prayed for.
SUES A FRESNO DENTIST
FOR $20000 DAMAGES
Tragic Outcome of a Suit for the
Hand of the Belle of
Bear Harbor. /
Special Dispatch to The Call.
COVELO, Aus. 7.â The traplc details of
a fatal affray between rival suitors at
Bear Harbor, a coast hamlet, reached
here by stage to-day. The principals in
the affray are Mattie Jensen. David
French and Benjamin Smith. French's
jealousy of Smith precipitated the quar
rel, and as a result French lies mortally
wounded with four bullets in his body,
while Smith is suffering from a serious
fracture of the skull received from the
iron bar in the hands of French.
It appears both men were rival suitors
for the hand of the young woman and
had been devoted in their attentions.
They were treated impartially and the
affection of Miss Mattie was equally di
vided. Both young men had proposed
n<arriage. but Miss Jensen had been un
able to choose between them. Both men
had been regular callers at the Jensen
house, though seldom If ever before both
appeared in the parlor together. Sunday
night last was Smith's visiting night, and
he was enjoying the society of his sweet
heart when a sharp knock at the parlor
door announced a caller. Smith opened
the door and was confronted by. French,
who asked that he might speak with him
a moment. Smith, not expecting any
trouble, unhesitatingly complied. The mo
ment he crossed the exit French struck
him a powerful blow with a heavy bar of
Iron, which prostrated him, and he fell
through the door Into the parlor in an
almost insensible condition.
Miss Jensen, when she noted the tide of
affairs, ran into an adjoining room and
procured a revolver and handed it to
Smith to protect himself. Smith instantly
grabbed the weapon and fired four shots
at French, all taking effect In vital parts
of the body. French is dying and Smith
is under arrest.

HIS SWEETHEART'S PISTOL

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early mornlns tides are riven in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are in addition to the soundings on the
Unltfd States Coast Survey charts, except
when a mlnusfglRn (â ) precedes the heleht.
and then the rtumber elven Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

On March 30th of this year the Board of
Supervisors of San Francisco passed Or-
dinance No. 25, prohibiting the burial of
the dead within the city and countv. Thta
law' goes Into effect August 1, * IBOL His-
tory shows that all large cities have found
It necessary to close their cemeteries, and
immediately the relatives and friends be-
gin to remove their loved orres. to cemeter-
ies less liable to be disturbed -by - the
growth of the city. For full particulars
and expenses In disinterring and transfer-
ring bodies apply to Charles H. r Jacob &
Co.. funeral directors and embalmers. 31S
Mason street. Telephone Main 6213.,

OAKLAND. CAL., August 7, 1900.
But in addition to the partisans of tho
two contending factions there is a large
mass of voters In Alameda County. It is
made up of men of tho better class and
of a high grade of Intelligence, and they
are the men who have an idea that the
County Clerk'* office is for the purpose of
conducting public business and not .for
a private campaign bureau.
Meanwhile the work of sending out cam
naisn literature and cards under ofllclal
Mr. Jordan Is equally particular to
Etate that whatever facilities he provided
for Henry P. Dalton this year he offered
to the managers of Mr. Metcalf, but he
has not expressed any opinion as to
whether they were* "smooth" enough to
take advantage of the offer.
So the County Clerk, despite his friend
ship for Mr. Metcalf two years ago and
his friendship for Mr Dalton this year,
asserts that he is Impartial in the use
of his office for "doing politics," and thai
the "other Bide" has always had an even
break at -the Great Register. And thus
the Alameda County Clerk's office be
comes an interesting study In the methods
of "doing" private politics without com
ing in dangerous conflict with the law.
So Mr. Jordan has succeeded In attract
ing to himself many of the bricks that
have been poised in the air ready for huri
lng at somebody ajid they are being hurled
fit the County Clerk. He has drawn tho
fire of both sides, for those who are using
him and his office to-day were against
him after the last primary, and those who
were, friendly to him then are against
him now.
At that time a series of long articles ap
peared in the Enquirer, which paper is
now supporting Jordan, attacking the
County Clerk for having issued certifi
cates, and particularly blank ones, over
the seal of his otilce and the signature of
his deputies.
These articles were signed simply, ine
Committee," and It waB inferred that they
were prepared by the campaign committee
<3f the defeated Congressman. They be
gan with charging the County Clerk with
Having issued fraudulent certificates, and
drew his denial. Then they charged that
blank certificates had been issued, and
this was met with a denial. Then those
who were preparing these articles and who
were having them published issued fac
similes of certificates issued by the Coun
ty Clerk, Including a blank certificate,
which had on it the seal of the County
Clerk and signature of J. B. Dean, one
of his deputies.
; As a result of these articles the name
I of the County Clerk was coupled in a
very unpleasant manner with the issu
ane'e of the documents, and Jordan's cer
tificates have f.one down into Alameda
County's political history.
So the tacts that were brought out at
the meeting of the Supervisors yesterday
were not altogether new. It was more
the fact that the County Clerk was con
fronted by his former friends as accusers
and that he was determined to threaten
them with the past in return for their
present charges that aroused interest.
The present condition of affairs is due
to the extreme bitterness that exists be
tween the factions in Alameda County,
and the high pitch of nervous excitement
to which the political managers have been
brought !n the last few weeks.
But bereft of its personal nature, the
matter simmers- down to the use of the
County Clerk's office for private political
ends. County Clerk Jordan took refuge
behind the statement that neither his acts
of two years ago for Victor H. Metcalf
nor his acts of the present year for Hen
ry P. Dalton were criminal, and his main
excuse was that whatever he did for one
6lde he had offered to do for j.he other
side.
Mr. Jordan was very particular to state
yesterday, and he Is very particular to
state to-day, that when he began the is
suance of the certificates for the Metcalf
managers two years ago he went to the
Hilborn managers and offered them the
6ame opportunity of issuing certltlcates,
but in the opinion of Mr. Jordan "Mr.
Hllborn's managers were not quite as
smooth as those of Mr. Metcalf."
The statements made by â¢ County
Clerk Jordan were not particularly
new. but It \*as the way In
which they were made and the con
ditions under which they were uttered
that made them of interest. The whole
matter was thrashed out a year and a
half ago. very shortly after Victor H.
Metcalf had been elected to Congress and
Frank C. Jordan had been elected County-
Clerk.
OAKLAND. Aug. 7. â County Clerk
Frank C. Jordan has succeeded in
turning the attention of the people
j of Alameda County from the Con

While Out at Ocean Beach Photo
graphing the Life Saving Crew
She Is Overcome by
the Breakers.
Miss Rose Tompkins, a teacher in the
Hawthorne Primary School, died yester
day morning in the Receiving Hospital
ambulance while being removed from the
ocean beach for treatment.
Miss Tompkins left her home at 1000
Van Ness avenue early yesterday morn
ing, saying she was going to the beach
to got a picture of the life-saving crew.
That was the last Miss Tompkins' aged
mother saw of .her until she heard of
her sudden demise, all the particulars of
which were given by the life-savers.
From the facts gleaned from the llfe
pavinpr crew it would appear that the in
structress, Intent on securing a good pic
ture and heedless of her surroundings,
was overtaken by the rolling surf and
knocked down. The water swept over
her and left her wet and dripplner and
prone upon the sands. The shock was
apparently too great for the weakened
condition of Miss Tompkins" heart, and
being unable to rise, another breaker
rushing in carried the unfortunate woman
out to sea. , .
The life-saving crew saw tne wave
strike Miss Tompkins and rushed to her
rescue. Willing hands carried her out of
the water and conveyed her to a point
far up on the beach. However, as the
lady was unconscious and all the usual
restoratives failed to bring her to. an am
bulance was hastily summoned. While
being- conveyed to the Receiving Hospi
tal Miss Tompkins passed away.
Miss Tompkins had been a valued mem
ber of the school department since 1S81.
She leaves a mother past S3 years of age
and a sister, Mrs. Llllie Lehmann, now
in Germany. When the awful news of
Miss Tompkins' death was broken to her
aged mother the poor old lady became
hysterical and the worst results are
feared.
Miss Tompkins is the daughter of the
late Judge Tompkins and numbered
among her friends some of the most
prominent families of the State. She was
a schoolmate of Lady Hesketh. formerly
Flora Sharon, and the warmest relations
are known to have existed between them.
The late teacher's mother is a wealthy
woman and her sister. Mrs. I^ehmann. Is
a woman of vast means. Miss Tomp
kins, however, desired to be independent
and chose teaching as a means to that
end. She was about 45 years of age.
Miss Rose Tompkins of Haw
thorne School Overcome
by Heart Failure.

The will bears date of September 13.
1S9S. and is witnessed by H. I,. King. 414
California street, notary public: George
T. Knox and A. Wolf of this city.
Henry A. and I>aura Voorman and H. L.
E. Myer, the executors, appeared in Judge
Coffey's court yesterday morning and re
quested that special letters of adminis
tration be issued to Henry A. Voorman.
These letters were made necessary, it
was represented, because of an Important
contract that was to mature within a few
weeks. The letters were granted and M.r.
Voorman was placed under $50,000 bonds.
His sureties are Mrs. Alice Tilden and
Mrs. Viola V. Willett.
The balance of the estate, after deduct
ing that to be paid to the housekeeper, is
equally divided among the children and
frandchildren of the decedent. Henry A.,
da and Laura Voorman are to receive
one-sixth of the estate; Viola AVlllett,
wife of W. M. Wlllett. Is to receive one
sixth, less $15,000. which has already been
paid her; Alice Tilden, wife of II. O. Til
den, is to receive one-sixth, less $13,000.
which has alreadv been paid her; and the
grandchildren, Henrietta Dubols and
Henry Dubols, are to receive one-twelfth.
Henry Dubols' share is to be held by the
executors in trust, the bequest to be re
ceived when he arrives at the age of 31
years. In the meantime he shall be paid
the Income derived from his share.
The total value of the estate cannot be
learned until the liabilities have been ac
counted for. It is said to be in the neigh
borhood of $500,000. The estate consists of
large real estate holdings in various sec
tions of the State, stocks, bonds, cash in
bank and a number of minor personal se
curities.
A peculiar feature of the will of the late
Henry Voorman, filed yesterday for pro
bate by Attorneys Lloyd & Wood, is
that no mention whatever Is made of Mr.
Voorman's wife. It reems she has been in
a sanitarium for a number of years. The
children have looked after their mother
and have seen that she received good
care. No provision, however, was made
by Mr. Voorman for assisting his sick
wife; In fact, her name is not once re
ferred to in the will. To his housekeeper,
Mrs. L. G. Beckford, he bequeathed the
sum of $10,000 '"for her faithfulness, care
and attention." Mrs. Beckford has been
with the family a great number of years,
nursing ail the children In their infancy.
The Balance of the Estate, Which Is
Estimated to Be Worth $5OO,
000, Goes to His Chil
dren.

Housekeeper, Mrs. L
Gr. Beckford.
